Column: 271

[Mr SPEAKER in the Chair]

ORAL ANSWERS TO QUESTIONS

COMMON MARKET TALKS
(Progress made)


    1. Mr Ong Chang Sam asked the Minister for Finance what progress has been made in the Common Market talks with the Federation of Malaya.

    The Parliamentary Secretary to the Minister for Finance (Mr S. Ramaswamy) (for the Minister for Finance): Mr Speaker. Sir, this House has been informed on several occasions that the discussions on a proposed Commodity Common Market between the Governments of the Federation of Malaya and Singapore have concluded with an agreement to submit the matter for further investigation by a body of international experts. That body of international experts, namely, the World Bank Mission headed by Professor Jaques Rueff, is now here and the two Governments are waiting to receive their preliminary recommendations.

    Mr Ong Chang Sam (Choa Chu Kang)(In Mandarin): Can the Parliamentary Secretary enlighten this House as to when this delegation from the World Bank can table a report to this House as regards its investigations?

Column: 272



    Mr Ramaswamy: The Word Bank Mission is here. They are making investigations and preparing their report.

    I cannot say what is the exact date when the report will be submitted.

Column: 272

TRADE BETWEEN SINGAPORE AND INDONESIA


    2. Mr Ong Chang Sam asked the Minister for Finance whether trade between Singapore and Indonesia has improved since the establishment of the Trade and Cultural Commission in Djakarta, and to what extent.

    Mr Ramaswamy (for the Minister for Finance): There has been a great improvement in total trade between Singapore and Indonesia since the establishment of the Singapore Trade and Cultural Representation. The value of Singapore's exports to Indonesia has more than doubled since 1960. In 1960, we exported $121 million worth of goods to Indonesia; in 1962, our exports increased to $292.5 million, which is 242 per cent of the 1960 figure.

    As the hon. Member may be aware, every country tries its utmost to expand its export trade and this increase in our exports to Indonesia is most gratifying.

    As regards imports, the value has declined and this is due principally to the heavy fall in the price of rubber over the last two to three years. Rubber accounts for more than three-fourths of our imports from Indonesia. Although values have dropped, the volume of imports, that is, the quantity of rubber imported, has increased considerably, from 285.3 thousand tons in 1960 to 367.2 thousand tons in 1962, an increase of nearly 30 per cent.

    The Singapore Trade and Cultural Representation has helped to explain Singapore's policy to the Indonesian Government and in return to understand and appreciate Indonesia's policy, thus helping to foster and improve cordial relations now existing between Singapore and Indonesia.

Column: 273

INVESTMENT OF GOVERNMENT
FUNDS IN FOREIGN COUNTRIES
SINCE 1962


    3. Mr Ong Chang Sam asked the Minister for Finance what amount of Government funds has been invested in foreign countries since 1962, and where.

    Mr Ramaswamy (for the Minister for Finance): I am sorry, Sir, for the delay in replying.

    Since 1st January, 1962, a total of ---

    Mr Speaker: Order. I think the Parliamentary Secretary is aware that if he is answering on behalf of a Minister, he should preface his remarks by saying, "I have been asked to reply."

    Mr Ramaswamy: Thank you, Mr Speaker, Sir, I have been asked to reply.

    Since 1st January, 1962, a total of $141 million was invested by the Singapore Government in gilt-edged securities available in the London money market. These Reserve Funds are not immediately required for utilisation in Singapore. The money so invested can, at a moment's notice, be transferred to Singapore any time the Government is in need of it. This practice of investing such reserves in the United Kingdom is adopted by all Commonwealth countries - Asian, and non-Asian.

Column: 273

PIONEER CERTIFICATES GRANTED SINCE 1959
(Amount of capital from foreign countries)


    4. Mr Ong Chang Sam asked the Minister for Finance how many pioneer certificates have been granted since June 1959; what is the total amount of paid-up capital involved; and how much of this capital is from foreign countries, and from which countries.

    Mr Ramaswamy (for the Minister for Finance): Mr Speaker, Sir, I have been asked to reply.

    Since June 1959, 24 pioneer certificates have been granted. Another five certificates will be granted soon.

Column: 274



    For the firms which have been awarded pioneer certificates, the capital involved is as follows:

Total Subscribed Capital     48,538,715

Total Authorised Capital      9,500,000

Total Subscribed Capital        920,202

Total Paid-up Capital           920,202

      (b)  Foreign              555,998


    Of the total amount of Paid-up Capital, 58 per cent is from abroad. This foreign capital comes from the United Kingdom, the United States of America, Japan, Hongkong, Holland, Switzerland, and other countries.

    Mr Ong Chang Sam(In Mandarin): Mr Speaker. Sir, the Parliamentary Secretary said that foreign capital of $19,139,998 has been invested in local industries, namely 58 per cent.

    Can he tell us whether the Government has any future plan to encourage our local capitalists to invest in our local industries and not to issue too many of these pioneer certificates to foreign capitalists?

    Mr Ramaswamy: We have always encouraged local capitalists to invest in Singapore.

Column: 274

BOGUS INSURANCE COMPANIES


    5. Mr Ong Chang Sam asked the Minister for Finance what action has been taken against bogus insurance companies since the coming into operation of the Life Assurance Companies (Compulsory Liquidation) Ordinance, 1962; and whether any policy holder has been refunded his money, and what percentage of the original amount.

    Mr Ramaswamy (for the Minister for Finance): Mr Speaker, Sir, I have been asked to reply.

Column: 275



    The Life Assurance Companies (Compulsory Liquidation) Ordinance, 1962, came into operation on 3rd April, 1962. Positive steps were then taken to wind up and liquidate the unsound life insurance companies in Singapore. There were, at that time, two groups of such companies. Firstly, Federation incorporated companies with branches in Singapore; and secondly, companies registered and incorporated in Singapore. The first group of insurance companies are being liquidated by order of the Federation High Court.

    The process of liquidating a company is a lengthy one, often complicated with legal technicalities. Because of this and because of the tens of thousands of claims that were reviewed, the liquidators in the Federation have not been able to complete their work in respect of any one company. There is information, however, that while a few companies may have some assets that could be realised and distributed, many were in poor financial position and it would be optimistic of any policy holder to expect any more than a fraction of his claims to be honoured.

    Action has been taken in Singapore to wind up the unsound insurance companies registered here. The liquidators appointed have been working to realise the assets of the insurance companies and of the three Singapore companies. Policy holders will be informed in the next month or two regarding their claims and the refunds they will receive.

    Mr Ong Chang Sam(In Mandarin): Mr Speaker, Sir, can the Parliamentary Secretary assure this House that after one or two months some of the policy holders can get a refund of their premiums?

    Mr Ramaswamy: Mr Speaker, I have already said that the policy holders will be informed in the next month or two regarding their claims and the refunds they will receive.

    Mr Ong Chang Sam(In Mandarin): Mr Speaker, according to the reply of the Parliamentary Secretary, it means

Column: 276

that the Government cannot assure this House that in the coming two or three months the policy holders cannot realise their claims.

    The Minister for Finance (Dr Goh Keng Swee) rose -

    Mr Speaker: The Parliamentary Secretary is answering questions.

    Mr Ramaswamy: Mr Speaker, Sir, it all depends on what assets the Companies have. If there are any assets, the policy holders will receive whatever can be distributed.

Column: 276

APPLICATIONS FOR INSERTION IN ELECTORAL REGISTERS
IN 1962


    6. Mr Ong Chang Sam asked the Minister for Home Affairs how many persons applied to have their names inserted in the electoral registers in 1962; how many applications have been approved; of these, how many were from Singapore citizens by birth, and how many were from Singapore citizens by registration; and how many have been refused, and the reasons for refusal.

    Mr Ong Pang Boon: Mr Speaker, Sir, it is presumed that the Member for Choa Chu Kang is referring to the claims submitted during the revision of the registers for 1962. There were roughly 15,000 claims submitted during the statutory period and the number of claims disallowed were 1,395. The reasons for the claims being disallowed were lack of qualification or non-attendance at the public inquiry to give information as regards entitlement to registration.

    Mr Ong Chang Sam(In Mandarin): Mr Speaker, Sir, the Minister said that 1,395 claims were refused. Can he inform this House as to what happened to the other 14,000 claims?

    Mr Ong Pang Boon: Mr Speaker, Sir, the answer is obvious. All the others except the 1,395 were approved and their names will be included in the registers for 1962.

Column: 277



    Mr Speaker: The question also asks how many of them were Singapore citizens by birth and how many were Singapore citizens by registration. Is the Minister prepared to answer that part of the question?

    Mr Ong Pang Boon: Mr Speaker, Sir, a compilation of these classified figures asked for by the Member for Choa Chu Kang has never been kept in the past and it is not being done now, especially when the Elections Department is busily engaged in the revision of the electoral registers for 1962.

    Mr Ong Chang Sam(In Mandarin): Mr Speaker, Sir, according to my knowledge, the registration of citizens born in Singapore is done by procuring their identity cards from the Identity Registry before they can be registered. Those who have been registered as citizens are those whose names have been supplied by the Citizenship Registry. Therefore, the Minister should be able to know the various figures asked for.

    Mr Ong Pang Boon: I cannot, for one moment, understand the purpose of the question of the Member for Choa Chu Kang. It is quite true that if the Government wants to get these figures, it can do so if there is time and the staff for it. As I said just now, the Elections Department is busy with the revision of the 1962 registers and, as far as I can see, there is no purpose in getting the figures.

    Mr Chan Sun Wing (Upper Serangoon)(In Mandarin): Mr Speaker, Sir, the question of those citizens born in Singapore and those registered as citizens arose in the constitutional talks between Singapore and the Federation. Therefore, this question is very pertinent. That is why we have asked this question. The reply given by the Minister shows that he is not willing to disclose these figures.

    Mr Speaker: The Member now is inviting a debate. Perhaps the Member's question could just be: In view of what

Column: 278

he has said, will the Minister reconsider his answer?

    Mr Ong Pang Boon: Mr Speaker, Sir, if a citizen by birth or by registration is qualified to be a voter, his name will be placed in the electoral register. I have just obtained information from the Minister for Finance that to obtain the information for the Member for Choa Chu Kang, the Government will require another $38,000.

    Mr Low Por Tuck (Havelock): Mr Speaker, Sir, according to the earlier reply of the Minister for Home Affairs, it seems that we are not entitled to know the figures or facts of the Government Ministry. I am asking, are we entitled to know the facts and figures in the Government Ministry?

    Mr Ong Pang Boon: Mr Speaker, Sir, if the Member for Havelock is prepared to approve $38,000 for this purpose, we will engage the staff to get this information for him.

    Mr Ong Chang Sam(In Mandarin): Mr Speaker, Sir, I move,

    That the sum to be allocated for Head 50 be reduced by the whole sum in respect of new subhead - National Referendum.

    Mr Speaker, Sir, under this item, the Government asks for $436,001 to be paid for the shameful National Referendum conducted on the 1st of September last year. Mr Speaker, Sir, to the people of Singapore, the 1st of September last year was a dark day. It was a day when parliamentary democracy died. Throughout the whole proxies of the National Referendum, the people in Singapore were able to see that the P.A.P., in order to prolong their political life, did all sorts of shameful things and utilised all means of the propaganda machinery to intimidate the people of Singapore. This is only for the sake of a few leaders in the oligarchy of the P.A.P. Mr Speaker, Sir, we saw that on that day

Column: 281

the Government used all police vans with loud-speakers. They went to every housing estate, every lane, every street and every village to intimidate people, to coerce them into voting for the Government's proposal as otherwise they would lose their citizenship rights. We were able to see that officers in the Ministry of Health went to the coffee shops, laundry shops and canteens. Now, if the licences of these shops had to be approved by the officers in the Ministry, then the officers went and pasted up Government posters about the National Referendum on the walls of these shops and intimidated the people into voting for proposal A, otherwise they would find difficulty in carrying on their business.

    Mr Speaker, Sir, you could also see that the Prime Minister in the mass meeting at Fullerton Square uttered his threats through Radio Singapore, thereby forcing the people to vote for the P.A.P.'s proposal. From all these propaganda activities conducted by the Government, we can see their main object was to salvage their political life and not for the national interest or for the interest of the people.

    Mr Speaker, Sir, you could also see that on National Referendum Day, when the voting came to an end, the various political Parties were not able to enjoy all the facilities. They were not allowed to send representatives to supervise the counting of the votes. We also saw that the ballot box in every division was not carefully examined and the representatives were not able to know where these ballot boxes came from. However, only the P.A.P. people knew to which electoral divisions these boxes belonged.

    Mr Speaker, Sir, this is very important. All the people in Singapore knew that the P.A.P. would make use of the National Referendum to find out the extent of the people's support for them. The Government also knew that if they openly disclosed the electoral boxes to the Opposition Parties, that would be to their disadvantage. Because of this, the whole National Referendum was conducted under undemocratic conditions.

Column: 282



    About three weeks ago when the Prime Minister paid a visit to Sembawang, he said during the course of the National Referendum that many people in the rural areas put in blank votes. That was because the Government was not doing enough in the field of propaganda in the rural areas. The Prime Minister also pointed out that during the course of the National Referendum, the number of blank votes in the Jurong area was very high. It was the highest throughout the whole of Singapore. Mr Speaker, Sir, all these speeches by the Prime Minister very clearly exposed their political aims in conducting this National Referendum. In other words, the Ministers in the P.A.P. were trying to seek out the safe seats in a future general election. Mr Speaker, Sir, that is why the Government spent the people's money for their own political objectives and they have no reason to ask the House to approve the $436,001 as ---

    The Chairman: The hon. Member is interpreted as saying that the amount to be voted is $436,001. The amount is $372,422.

What I have said just now very clearly shows that we the people in Singapore have no obligations to pay out this sum of money because the people of Singapore were not able to know the detailed results of the voting in each electoral division except the P.A.P. In view of this, therefore, the Government have no reason to ask the people to defray this amount of expenditure. Mr Speaker, Sir, that is why I think this sum of money should not be approved by this House.

    Mr Chan Sun Wing(In Mandarin): Mr Speaker, I deem it necessary to add to what my colleague has said just now. This is a question of principle. No matter what amount of money was spent on the National Referendum, it should not be paid by the people in Singapore, because in the 51 electoral divisions, how many people voted for proposal A, how many people voted for proposal B, how many people voted for proposal C, how many people cast blank votes, how many spoilt votes were there, and how many people did not turn up at the polling stations? All this information about the voting was turned into a political secret by the P.A.P. and was known only to the P.A.P. Therefore, this amount of money should be defrayed by the P.A.P. and not paid by the taxpayers because the Members in this House are all elected by the people. Therefore, if the expenses were to be borne by the people, then the representatives in this House should be entitled to know the results of the referendum. But we are not able to know the detailed results of each electoral division, and if the P.A.P. kept this information as their political secret, then they should pay for it.

3.15 p.m.

    We know that if they want to buy political secrets, they have to pay more. Therefore, they should pay a sum which doubles this amount if they want to buy political secrets. We are not objecting to their buying these political secrets. So they have to pay for this and not ask the people to pay for it. The crux of the problem is not whether the amount is great or small but whether or not the result of the referendum should be made known to the people. Therefore, the people in Singapore have no obligation to pay for this amount of money.

item of expenditure. Since he chose to raise his objections on purely political grounds. I feel that in defence of the Supplementary Estimates before the House I must meet him on the same grounds. And so I crave your indulgence, Mr Speaker, Sir, if I should ---

    Dr Goh Keng Swee: Yes, I do not dispute that, Mr Speaker, Sir. But it does occur to me that in the course of my rebuttal, I may have to go over very tedious arguments which have been belaboured in this House so often and much to your discomfiture and perhaps tedium. So in advance, Mr Speaker, Sir, I do crave your indulgence if I have to ---

    Mr A. P. Rajah (Farrer Park): Make a two-hour speech?

    Dr Goh Keng Swee: One hour really. Sir, the Member for Choa Chu Kang said that the 1st of September last year was really a dark day for democracy and that, in fact, he saw the referendum was an exercise of such evil dimensions that it was a very gloomy day for democracy. Mr Speaker, Sir, I cannot at all subscribe to this sentiment. Far from 1st September being a dark day for democracy, it was the brightest day, it was the turning point for democracy in Singapore, for it was on that day that the Communists, pro-Communists and their sympathisers working in the open front organisations were completely exposed. Until then, they had tried to build a myth of invincibility - that they control the masses, that they control the trade unions, and that anybody who stood up against them would be destroyed politically through the democratic processes. But eventually when they take over the organs of State, the destruction will be more than just a question of electoral constitutions; it will be physical elimination and liquidation. That was the fear, Mr Speaker, Sir, that they would spread through all their multifarious open front organisations

Column: 285

from the time they broke away from the P.A.P. in May 1961. These are the weak men who themselves were for the most part not believers in Marxism-Leninism as a revolutionary philosophy; but when the parting of ways took place between the non-Communist socialists in the P.A.P. and the Communists and pro-Communists in the P.A.P., these men and women were made of straw. Subjected to a tremendous amount of persuasion and intimidation, they decided to "play with the Communists". That was the only reason why they weakened and went over to the other side, thinking that, with all the panoply of open front organisations which we have allowed the Communists to organise, it was only a matter of time when they could seize power and break us up. And we took up this challenge, and on 1st September last year the battle was joined. What was the result? We allowed them all the constitutional freedoms - they could have all the mass meetings provided they observed the laws, which they did. I must pay them a tribute, Mr Speaker, Sir. The mass meetings were held in accordance with the conditions of the licences stated for the conduct of such mass meetings, and they thought that they were winning. And this is the curious thing - almost to the end the Communists and their running dogs in the Barisan Sosialis believed that they were going to win. Never for one moment, until perhaps a day before polling day, did they entertain any doubt at all as to the outcome of the referendum; so certain were they!

    Now, Sir, why were they routed? Why was there such a tremendous defeat for these people?

    Sir, if you will bear with me for a moment, I will try to analyse the position. As I said, I have got to go back to basic historical processes when the Communists broke with us. Their No. 1 mistake was to break with the P.A.P. on a national issue. We said that we wanted merger with Malaya and that we were in favour of Malaysia, as indeed all genuine nationalists in this country must support. They oppose `merger and Malaysia for obvious reasons which have been explained adnauseam

Column: 286

in this House. They oppose merger and Malaysia simply because after merger and Malaysia the Internal Security Council will be in the hands of the Federation Government who have a less delicate regard for constitutional niceties and rights that can be afforded to Communists and their fellow-travellers. The Federation Government have got clear views on them. We, on the other hand, say we will always give them an opportunity for the legal open confrontation so long as they observe the laws and the constitution of the country. But I am afraid opinion in Kuala Lumpur differs from ours on that point. Therefore, because they feel that after merger and Malaysia they will be conscribed, they oppose it purely for their own selfish political interests. That is their No. 1 error which led to their defeat on 1st September.

    Their No. 2 mistake, which combined with their No. 1 mistake proved fatal, was that they said they wanted more merger than the P.A.P. They wanted complete merger. Therefore, since we wanted merger on special terms and they wanted complete merger, the issue that had to be posed in this referendum was not whether you wanted merger or you did not want merger, but what type of merger did you want. Our type of merger was Alternative A; complete merger Alternative B; or the Borneo/Sarawak type of merger Alternative C. Those were the three honest issues that were posed to the electorate for their choice. I do not want to go through the merits of each of the three alternative choices; but suffice it to say, Mr Speaker, Sir, that the electorate of Singapore listened to all points of view. We never stopped the Barisan from having mass meetings. They were completely free to disseminate their views any time they liked, in any way they liked, provided they observed the law. And they went pretty close to the brink. For instance, carrying out this phoney Gallup Poll in Tanjong Pagar and Kreta Ayer - strictly speaking, according to the Municipal bye-laws and a few local laws, these exercises were illegal. But we said. "Well, it does not matter." They wanted

Column: 287

radio time and we offered them radio time. We wrote to them, "Please come to Radio Singapore and let us discuss matters in a human and adult manner." Mr Speaker, Sir, I am sad to relate that after about ten days of the open confrontation before the radio microphone, the Barisan decided that they had enough of it and would not appear before Radio Singapore.

    The people knew exactly what was fought for, Mr Speaker, Sir. No amount of sophistry, of half-cooked Marxism-Leninism or what-have-you on the other side can deceive the people that the best interest in the near future is to have a limited conditional form of merger whereby Singapore will have a considerable measure of local autonomy. And because the Barisan were foolish enough to try to outbid the P.A.P. in wanting more merger than we did, they failed in the result because the people of Singapore at the present moment are prepared to go only so far and no further in a merger with the Federation of Malaya. The Barisan Sosialis thought that complete merger was out of the question. In fact, Mr Speaker, Sir, if I may let the House into the secret for the first time as to the reason why the Barisan Sosialis tried to outbid the P.A.P. in demanding complete merger. Mr James Puthucheary was an old colleague of ours. In 1959 we unlocked the gates of Changi Gaol and released him and placed him in a position of responsibility. When the fight between the P.A.P. and the pro-Communists developed into a crisis, I could see Mr James Puthucheary - he was working in my Ministry - weakening from day to day. Eventually he told me that he was going over to the other side as he found his position as a supporter of the P.A.P. intolerable. And he said they were going to wreck merger, "This is absolutely on the cards." I said to him, "James, why do you say so?" Then he did some calculations on a piece of paper. He said, "There are 105 seats in the Federal Parliament. The Alliance has 60 votes; the Opposition has 40 votes; a margin of 20. If Singapore were to go in on full merger, Singapore would get

Column: 288

anything between 20 and 25 votes, the majority of which would be anti-Alliance votes. Therefore, under no circumstances will the Federation Government contemplate complete merger." Complete merger, of course, means proportionate representation. Therefore, he said, "We are going to outbid you, and that will put you in a fix."

    That was the No. 1 reason. I do not know if that was the No. 1 or No. 2 reason - the strategist in the Barisan Sosialis who led them up the garden path. It was absolutely a basic error in political strategy. If at that time in May or June 1961 they had said, "Nonsense with merger. Absolutely nothing. We want an independent Singapore, come what may", then, Mr Speaker, Sir, if we had a referendum, the choice must be either merger with the Federation on those terms or complete independence. And if the alternative posed in the referendum was either merger with the Federation or complete independence for Singapore, the result could have been one way or the other. But given a choice of limited and conditional merger, and complete merger, we had no doubt at all what the result would be - a shattering defeat for the pro-Communists who on that occasion were trying to be too clever.

3.30 p.m.

    Now, Sir, although we had no doubt as to the outcome, we took the precaution of ensuring that the electorate understood all the basic issues involved in the referendum. The Barisan accused us of using the radio to put forward these views. What is so wrong about it? What is the radio for but to explain to the people the nature of the decisive choice which they had to make on 1st September? It was quite proper. We had to do it. We have no apologies at all. We are not at all ashamed. In. fact, we are very proud and we are very glad that they found out how the use of the radio was so effective. That is why we have advanced the time for starting Television. It is going to be even more effective - not only sound, but audio-visual aids!

Column: 289



    Mr Speaker, Sir, we do not really enjoy tormenting the young men and women on the other side, but I think it is about time they listened to a few home-truths, having wandered, like the lost tribes, through the desert and not being able to come out. Now, Sir, not oily did we use the radio but we made sure - after all, not every family in Singapore owns a radio set - that there had to be a face-to-face explanation. The Member for Choa Chu Kang complained about the use of police cars. We used police cars simply because - I have forgotten the exact number; I think there were about eight or nine police cars - we could commandeer them at the time for use to explain to the people, face to face. We told them what this was all about. We used police cars because some of them have loud-speaker equipment. If the Barisan Sosialis think that the whole of our field propaganda outfit consists of police cars, they are absolutely wrong. The police cars amounted to more than 10 per cent of the loud-speaker vehicles which we sent out. We sent out 75 such vehicles equipped with radio, loud-speaker and tape-recorder. On the average, each of these teams carried out between 30 and 50 ï¿½-hour meetings per day, bringing a total, during the referendum period, of between 6,000 and 8,000 meetings throughout the towns, streets and villages. Let them not believe that this is just a matter of half a dozen police cars. We arranged the referendum publicity campaign with all the thoroughness and energy that we could command. There is absolutely no apology for what we did.

    Now, the broadcasting of Fullerton Square speeches. What is wrong with that? I do not know why they even raised that subject. But on the fourth point which they raised, that is, the result of the referendum was not known to the Barisan. Sosialis, they complained, saying, "You must know what the results are."

    Mr Speaker, Sir, the referendum was conducted on the 1st of September. I thought the result was published on the

Column: 290

2nd of September last year. I am surprised that they do not know the result. The result was that they had a hell of a whacking.

    An hon. Member: Not by constituency.

    Dr Goh Keng Swee: Ah, they want to know the result constituency by constituency. I would have thought that for their peace of mind, it is better that they do not know. To these two particular Assemblymen, for their own peace of mind, I say, the less said, the better. They want to know! Why should they know? The law does not say that they must know. No referendum results in any democratic country, or even in any undemocratic country, are published constituency by constituency. You will remember, Mr Speaker, Sir, we went through all this in Select Committee. We challenged the representors in Select Committee to produce any precedent, and they were completely unable to do so.

    The Member for Upper Serangoon said that the P.A.P. know the result and that therefore we should pay for it. This really brings into the proceedings of this Assembly a somewhat mercenary element which we do not quite like. But I say to the Member for Upper Serangoon, whether or not the Barisan want to pay for it, they will still not know what the detailed results are. There is absolutely no reason why they should know. In fact, for their own peace of mind, it is better that they should not know.

    "That the following Address in reply to the speech of the Yang di-Pertuan Negara be agreed to:-

3.43 p.m.

    Inche Ahmad Jabri Bin Mohammad Akib (Southern Islands)(In Malay): Mr Speaker, Sir, to continue with my speech where I left off yesterday in connection with the motion in the name of the Prime Minister expressing our thanks to the Yang di-Pertuan Negara. I was speaking of the awakening on the part of the Government to expect co-operation and support of all political leaders and community leaders. However, from the practices employed by the Government, I can say that they still consider the Opposition Members as a group which cannot extend their co-operation in the interest of the people of Singapore. I say as a Member of this Assembly, as an elected representative of the people, that it is accepted in the Constitution of this State that each representative represents his respective constituency irrespective of whether the constituents voted for him or not. However, in the light of the

Column: 293

tactics adopted by the Government, it does not even consider such representatives as elected representatives, let alone extend respect to them. For instance, I am unable to understand the concept of the P.A.P. democracy when, as the representative of the Southern Islands, I was not consulted on the occasion of the visit of the Head of State to the Southern Islands. I feel that it should have been proper on the part of the Government to have consulted me.

    Later, Mr Speaker, the Prime Minister will visit the rural constituencies throughout Singapore. I understand the Prime Minister has visited the Kampong Kembangan constituency where the representative is my colleague. I was informed that the `Member for Kampong Kembangan was only invited to participate just for name's sake only. I understand that the Prime Minister will be visiting my constituency - the Southern Islands - either on the 21st or the 28th. This came to my knowledge unofficially. Meetings were conducted by the heads of the Islands to prepare for the Prime Minister's visit. At these meetings, the question was asked why the Member for the Southern Islands was left out at the meetings to discuss the Prime Minister's visit. The Government's reply was that the object of the proposed visit is for the Prime Minister to know the extent of the difficulties encountered by the people in this constituency. The question which I have posed, Mr Speaker, is not motivated by sheer jealousy for fear of loss of my constituency, but it was motivated more by our attempt to achieve a democratic society. I think the time When we have different views on political ideologies was during election time. It is up to the people to make their choice according to the Constitution. In accordance with the practice of parliamentary democracy, the candidate who gets the most votes is elected. Surely the intention is not to carry on this ideological battle continuously. We should not engage in activities which will confuse the people. If we are sincere and are dedicated to the service of the people, we should not have any qualms or fear as to whether we may or may not be elected at the next

Column: 294

election. That is the accepted practice of parliamentary democracy. We will have more responsibilities to shoulder in the next 4ï¿½ months. If this practice based on party ideology is carried on, as expressed by the Member for Geylang Serai, there is bound to be that feeling of provincialism when Malaysia is achieved. This will lead to a state of affairs which no one wishes. As unanimously agreed, we should set an example to fulfil our duties to our people.

    Mr Speaker, I further wish to touch upon the Communist activities as mentioned in the speech delivered by His Excellency. We must be ready to face any eventuality which may be posed by the Communist threat in future. It is appropriate that we should shoulder this responsibility. We cannot face this responsibility all by ourselves. When we realise the manoeuvres of our enemies arc to wreck our hopes, we should not' hesitate and be afraid to take positive steps which we are certain will bring prosperity and security to our State. As I have said in this House on a number of occasions, we have shown the dangers and yet the Government fights shy of taking action, or is passing the buck to the others to take action - I cannot say which. If the P.A.P. Government, which is non-Communist, is shy to declare openly that it is anti-Communist but sincerely believes that the Communists have no sympathy towards humanity and are constantly taking destructive action, I hope it will really behave like non-Communists. I hope it will not keep, in its midst pro-Communist elements who are still at large in Singapore. Mr Speaker, this matter will be mentioned again in the next motion. I do not intend now to make a lengthy speech.

Column: 295

set aside so that we will not be bothered by that.

    Regarding the future policy of the Government, I would like to mention the treatment given by the Government for equal opportunities. I think the Malays in Singapore are ready to live in harmony with the other communities, but the Government's hope to establish an egalitarian society will not be achieved. I respect the P.A.P. Government with its socialistic policies. But it has been stressed in the Constitution, as mentioned by the Member for Geylang Serai, that it should be the responsibility of any government to promote the interests of the Malays in all spheres. The object of including this clause in the Preamble is not because the Malays want to supersede the other races. It is but a request for the non-Malays to consider the special position of the indigenous people so that the Malays will not be treated like the Red Indians in America and the Maoris in New Zealand. What harm is there in the Government helping the Malays to lift up their standard of living irrespective of their political beliefs? I have to mention this, Mr Speaker, because this has been misrepresented as a result of different political beliefs. There are political Parties who are afraid to implement this clause in the Preamble. For instance, if there are non-Malay leaders, such leaders are not prepared to put forward a scheme for the Malays because the non-Malays will not support their political Parties in future. If there are Malays voicing the special position of the Malays, those Malays will be accused of indulging in communalism. Mr Speaker, the awakening of the Malays to survive and to live has been proved by their acceptance of the Constitution. If they are hard pressed and there is no change in their status-for instance, the provision of flats. The Malays are not accustomed to living in flats. If it is within the means of the Malays to live in flats, there is no doubt that they will be prepared to live in them. On the other hand, if he is a fisherman or a hawker

Column: 296

earning $2 or $3 a day, surely he cannot afford to live in and pay a rental for a flat. The Housing and Development Board expect that rentals for flats must be paid promptly, otherwise the tenants will be evicted. This aspect must be considered.

    We know the environments here are pleasant, but in the Southern Islands there is really a sorry state of affairs, to the extent that talented children, who are keen to further their studies and who have passed their examinations, cannot go to secondary school because their parents cannot afford to send them to secondary school in Singapore. In the circumstances, the parents have no alternative but to stop their children from attending secondary school in Singapore. I have on many occasions pressed the Government to build a secondary school in the Southern Islands for the benefit of the children there. Unfortunately, my request has not been considered by the Government. I do not know if it is because they consider the request as coming from a non-P.A.P. member. I have made arrangements for those children attending primary school to live on the mainland. I hear the officials of the Ministry are not very happy with my action because they consider it is a reflection on the Government. In all sincerity, Mr Speaker, I am prepared to assist these unfortunate children in any way I can to make available facilities for their further education. For instance, the school at Blakang Mati. In 1961 I raised this question. The Minister for Education himself, when asked when the school at Blakang Mati was going to be put up, replied that it would be in 1961. But it is not until 1963 that the school is put up. This is also to coincide with the forthcoming visit of the Prime Minister to the Southern Islands. Similarly, the jetty at Sekijang Pelepah. Because the Prime Minister will be visiting that island, work is now in hand to repair the jetty. The school at Sekijang Pelepah, which was built when the Member for Hong Lim was the then Minister for National Development, is being left haunted. These are all political manoeuvres and there is no sincere intention to bring facilities

Column: 297

to the Southern Islands. We should not continue with these practices as political leaders; we should forget political affiliations when we fulfil our obligations towards the people. We should not allow the people to be made use of as political pawns.

    On the question of .the Economic Development Board which is responsible for the economic development of Singapore, many sites have been prepared for industrial growth. One of the islands affected is Pulau Ayer Limau.

4.11 p.m.

    Mr Deputy Speaker, I learnt that from the newspapers and I immediately wrote to the Board about the plight of the people. In our endeavours to industrialise, I bad hoped that the Malays who have to be resettled on Pulau Semulut would also benefit. I had also hoped that this would pave the way for them to be in gainful employment. Hitherto, they are engaged in fishing. The setting up of factories in the vicinity will, it is hoped, mean more employment for the islanders. However, it appears that the islanders are still waiting for the setting up of factories and I feel guilty as though I have deceived them. When I got their consent to shift them from Pulau Semulut to Pulau Ayer Limau, I gave them an assurance that they would be considered for employment when the factories are set up. They are still waiting and there is not a single factory that has issued a notice to the islanders asking whether they are prepared to work in the factories. Very few people indeed have got employment. The Board has never recruited any hands from the islands. If there is an intention to get these people to live in flats, and if they are unemployed, how can we expect them to do so unless, of course, they are in steady employment?

Although it has been mentioned that the Government has provided free textbooks and free schooling, it is still found that the agents of Government or Government officials do not give consideration to the special position of the Malays. For instance, on the subject of circulars from Ministries regarding the provision of textbooks, some teachers have failed to notify the students of this facility for the reason that if they do so it means more work for them. Such circulars which the teachers receive from the Ministry are not relayed to the students. For instance, if certain forms issued by the Ministry are in the English language, the parents cannot understand these forms. So time is wasted in their translation. The excuse given by the teachers is that the applications submitted cannot be considered as they are too late. This is the method employed by the Ministry. I think it must be rectified. It is a slow process to build a just and equal society.

when he moves into a fiat, let us say, if he takes a one-room flat and pays about $25; only four or five persons can stay in that flat? Or whether the need for extra money and the opportunities made available by the new housing provision have led to the economic independence of the women? The necessity to work and the opportunities available might have made the women go out and work. Therefore, it is a good thing and Government should encourage this. How and to what extent the modern provision of housing affects and disturbs the patterns of family and community life? Whether modern housing patterns promote racial harmony? Or do they have the opposite effect of causing friction among people of different races, beliefs, customs and creeds? 5.53 p.m.

    Mr Rajah: Mr Deputy Speaker, there are one or two points which I wish to bring to the attention of the House. The Prime Minister in his speech said that private enterprise, so far as building in Singapore was concerned, was estimated by the Government to contribute at least 6,000 units per annum during the course of the ensuing year. And I wish to bring to the notice of the House a problem which intensive private development is bringing about in Singapore, and, unless it is checked, will lead to great difficulties in the future. Private development now confines itself to building multi-storey flats to a very large extent and selling each of these flats to people in the middle income groups. Now, it raises a problem of management of these houses, a problem of ownership

Column: 317

in the future, and the problem of maintenance and upkeep of these flats and of the block in which the flats are situated. The Housing Board builds these flats and lets them out to private individuals. But while they are let out, the Housing Board sees to it that the passages and stairways are properly maintained, lighted and looked after. So far as these private flats are concerned, they arc now being sold by the hundreds to the middle income group people. The private developer is a private limited company. The point I wish to stress, Mr Deputy Speaker, is this: that these are all private limited companies, and in the conveyances which they give to the purchasers of these flats, these companies give certain covenants, certain undertakings to the purchasers of these flats, such as that they would maintain the stairways, see that they are lighted, that they are repaired and so on and so forth, which makes it possible for the flat-dwellers to enjoy living in those flats. Now it has already come to our notice that some of these private limited companies which started, say, five or six years previous to today have wound themselves up. They have wound themselves up and disappeared from the scene and have ceased to be legal entities. `The net result now is that some of the people who bought the houses from them do not have anybody against whom they can proceed in case the covenants they have undertaken to carry out are not implemented. But if it is a private person, you can either go against him or against his estate. But if it is a private limited company and once it goes into liquidation, it ceases to exist, and there is nobody against whom you can proceed. I bring this problem to the attention of Government who are now taking a liberal view with regard to houses which are owner-occupied. I think one of the results of that attitude is going to be an even more intensive development of flats by private companies with a view to idling them to the middle income groups. My own view, Mr Deputy Speaker, is that these private limited companies which go in for housing development should be treated in a different category, and separate legislation should

Column: 318

be brought about whereby they could be kept under proper control, so that these middle income group purchasers, in five or ten years' time, will not be left high and dry with their stairways rotting, their roofs leaking, and all the covenants not being able to be implemented. That is the first point I thought I should bring to the notice of Government.

    The next point, Mr Deputy Speaker, on which I wish to address the House is the question of the water supply of Singapore, on which the Prime Minister spoke. The Prime Minister has indicated in his speech that during the last three years there has been an increase of 9 million gallons in the consumption of water, so that the yearly increase at the moment is 3 million gallons.

    The Prime Minister has given figures that the consumption of water today is 73 million gallons per day. He has also indicated that so far as Tebrau is concerned, he can get a maximum intake into Singapore of 45 million gallons per day, and that the rest of the water supply for every day conies from the impounding reservoirs. He has also indicated that in the impounding reservoirs there is only 54 per cent of the maximum supply. The position, to my mind, is a very serious one, particularly as the Government is pressing on with the building of flats. The more flats that are built, the more water will be consumed. And if the Government builds another 12,000 houses, as they say they will, then the consumption will most assuredly rise beyond 3 million gallons next year. If the scheme in Jurong materialises in the way the Minister for Finance hopes that it will, and if the steel mill in Jurong goes up, in the way we hope it will, the steel mill alone will account for one million gallons per day.

    The Government has suggested that it wil1 build some sort of a natural reservoir in Jurong. How far that project has progressed Government has not informed us, although schemes are afoot, it seems. Whether the reservoir will be in readiness to receive the steel mill is another matter. All in all, Mr Deputy Speaker, the position with regard to

Column: 319

the water supply is a very grave one, and I regret that the Government does not seem to be unduly worried about the position, because it seems to be taking its time over augmenting our water supply. It has started a scheme at Scudai, and it says that under the Scudai scheme it hopes to get in about 15 million gallons per day. It says that that river will be brought into operation, so far as our water supply is concerned, towards the end of next year.

    Now, the position with regard to Scudai and Tebrau is this: these two rivers are tidal rivers and they are subject to a dry weather flow and a wet weather flow. When the Prime Minister says he can get a maximum of 45 million gallons per day, I think what he really means to tell the House is that from the river at its wettest, he will get 45 million gallons per day. But when Tebrau is at its driest, he cannot get more than 10 million gallons per day. Similarly Scudai is also subject to a dry and wet weather flow, and when it is at its driest, he cannot get from it more than 5 million gallons per day. Assume a very dry season, such as we might run into in the course of the next few months, the result will be that even with Tebrau and Scudai working to its full dry weather capacity, we could have a water shortage in Singapore, and we will, Mr Deputy Speaker. I regret very much that the Government is not pressing on as fast as it can with the Johore River scheme, and I regret that the first intake from the Johore River, according to the Prime Minister, will not be until the year 1967, which is 4 years away. Unless and until we can get to Johore as quickly as possible, we shall always be faced with the danger of a water shortage in Singapore, Nobody likes to predict ill, but, unless we get to the Johore River as quickly as possible, we will always be faced with the danger of a shortage of water supply during the dry season in Singapore. The dry season in Singapore also coincides with the dry season in the south of Johore where these two rivers are situate.

Column: 320

something for the people and they have not been able to deny that we, the P.A.P., have done a lot for the people. This is worthy of our attention. Why do they not say the P.A.P. is incompetent? They know that this thesis is false. It is one more mass medium - and a very powerful medium - with which to combat and circumscribe the Communists in the open encounter.

    We in the Government realise that there is nothing the Communists dread more than the open encounter, the open argument. During the past two years the argument, particularly over Malaysia and merger, was carried out with the Communists in the newspapers and radio. Members will remember how we invited the Communists and their front men to radio forums which at first they accepted with alacrity believing that here at last was a chance to put forward their case to hundreds of thousands of people. But the first encounter in which the Member for Queenstown participated proved to be such a disaster for the Communist cause that subsequent invitations to such forums were very reluctantly accepted and then finally, consistently rejected. With television, the Member for Queenstown knows full well not only will he be heard but seen as well. And to this extent, he knows that he will inflict twice and probably thrice as much damage to his cause as he was able to inflict by his radio appearance. That is why I thought he looked a little bit disconcerted yesterday when the Prime Minister extended an invitation for him to participate in a television forum. I advise the Member for Queens-town that he can do no better service to the cause of non-Communist socialist democracy than by appearing in a television forum and pleading the cause of the pro-Communist Barisan.

    Let me state here that the battle against Communism and anti-national forces is as much one of restraining their propensity to resort to violence as it is a battle of ideas. In the battle of ideas, mass media like radio, television are vital, and the Government intends to use them as effectively as possible to safeguard the democratic system as well as the security of Malaysia. The

Column: 339

Barisan Sosialis and their Communist mentors know that we can use this mass media to counter their propaganda effectively in this battle with the Communists. Recently, the battle over merger and Malaysia was largely a propaganda battle, sustained and relentless, but in the end, we showed that the non-Communist socialists can win this propagandas battle ---

    Mr Speaker: Order. The Leader of the House wishes to move a business motion.

EXEMPTED BUSINESS AND ADJOURNMENT TO A LATER
DAY MOTION


7.15 p.m.

    The Deputy Prime Minister (Dr Toh Chin Chye): Mr Speaker, I beg to move,

    Notwithstanding the resolution of the Assembly on Monday, 8th April, 1963 -

    (a) that this Assembly do sit after 12 midnight today;

    (b) that the proceedings on item 4 on the Order Paper be exempted from the provisions of Standing Order No. 1;

    (c) that at the conclusion of the proceedings on item 4 on the Order Paper, this Assembly do stand adjourned to 7th June, 1963.

    Mr Speaker, in explanation of this motion, I would like to inform the House that yesterday the House passed a motion approving that at the rising of the Assembly today we adjourn to the 7th of June, 1963. As Members of the House are aware, 12 o'clock midnight is the end of Tuesday, and it would, therefore, be impossible for this Assembly to sit after 12 o'clock midnight. In order to remove this anomaly, we have therefore moved this motion.

    Mr Speaker: A motion of this nature would really have needed notice, but it can be moved under the provisions of Standing Order 99 if this House assents, ff there is one negative voice, then the motion cannot be moved, is it the pleasure then of this Assembly that the motion be moved?

Column: 340



    No dissentient voice. I will therefore propose the Question. The motion is debatable.

    Question proposed.

    [No Member wishing to speak.]

    Question put, and agreed to.

    Resolved,

    Notwithstanding the resolution of the Assembly on Monday, 8th April, 1963-

    (a) that this Assembly do sit after 12 midnight today;

    (b) that the proceedings on item 4 on the Order Paper be exempted from the provisions of Standing Order No. 1;

    (c) that at the conclusion of the proceedings on item 4 on the Order Paper, this Assembly do stand adjourned to 7th June, 1963.

7.20 p.m.

    Mr Rajaratnam: As I said, Sir, the battle against the Communists is a battle of ideas, and yesterday the Member for Queenstown paid unwitting tribute to the efficacy of the democratic propaganda against Communist propaganda during the Malaysia battle. He said that the Government was pressing ahead with plans to have more radios and T.V. sets in the rural areas because the blank votes were higher in the rural areas. This is a correct appraisal of the situation. In the urban areas, thanks to fuller coverage by radio, newspapers and Rediffusion, the open argument between the democratic forces and the Communists reached the ears of the ordinary men who, having heard both sides of the case, rejected the Communist case in favour of the democratic case. In the rural areas, however, Communist propaganda was pressed through such outfits as the Country People's Association and the Rural Dwellers' Association. The democratic case did not reach the ears of the rural people as widely as the Communist case, or rather Communist rumours and falsehoods. So the Member

Column: 341

for Queenstown knows that once T.V. and radio are introduced into the rural areas as well, we can not only begin to nail down every Communist falsehood and rumour but bring home to the rural people the simple truth, that the democratic system can give them a better life. Therefore, this violent dislike for television and radio by the Communists means that we have done the right thing in establishing these new mass media and it would certainly be one big nail in the Communist coffin.

    The Barisan Sosialis may also be interested to know that towards the latter part of the year, a second television channel will be established, and next year a third channel and television, like other mass media, will be effective allies of democracy against the Barisan Sosialis and their Communist manipulators. Unable, therefore, to offer serious criticism of the achievements of the Government, the Barisan Sosialis leaders do not know what to do. All they can offer by way of improvement to the motion on the Yang di-Pertuan Negara's speech is an amendment "to truthfully and honestly ascertain the views of the people on their constitutional future". This is, I think, one of the most astonishing cases of collective amnesia that I have come across, because many of us will remember that some eight months ago, on 1st September, the people of Singapore went to the polls to decide on their constitutional future. They had to decide whether they wanted merger with autonomy in education and labour, or complete merger, or merger on terms available to the Borneo territories. I distinctly remember the Barisan Sosialis running around telling the people that all these three alternatives were bad and that the people of Singapore should show their disapproval of these three alternatives by casting blank votes. We all know that the outcome was that over 70 per cent of the people voted for alternative A, that is, merger with autonomy, and only some 25 per cent east blank votes. We know that the result came as a shock to the Communists, and also to the Member for Hong Lim, who also had been running around for reasons of his own asking the people to

Column: 342

cast blank votes. The fact that the Communists got only 25 per cent of the votes exploded the Communist myth which they got even many non-Communists to believe, that the Communists had unassailable influence over the masses of Singapore. This myth of the mass influence of the Communists was often used to neutralise the non-Communists with the threat that if they openly opposed the Communists, then they would be dealt with when the Communists won the next general election. So the fact that the Communists were able to influence only 25 per cent - and even that percentage must be shared with the Member for Hong Lim because he also campaigned for blank votes; so if we give him 5 per cent, then at best the Communists were able to influence only 20 per cent of the electorate to follow their line. And as a result of the explosion of this myth of Communist mass influence, today more and more people are prepared to stand up to the Communists. The power of Communist intimidation has been considerably weakened as a result of the exposure of this myth of Communist mass influence.

    Even more disastrous for the Communists has been the impact of this defeat on the weaker of their Communist supporters. These weaker of their Communist supporters, these weaker comrades, had trailed after the Communists in the fond hope that they were on the winning side, that they were with a Party which had the majority of the masses behind it. But now the Party which could only mobilise 25 per cent of the electorate, and that by and large from the rural areas, could no longer hold the old fascination for the weaker followers of the Communist cause. So, therefore, one myth having been destroyed - the myth of Communist mass influence - it became necessary for the Communists and the Barisan Sosialis in the interest of its own survival to create yet another new myth to repair the damage and prevent demoralisation among their dwindling supporters. Therefore, the new myth is now being developed that the defeat that the Communists suffered at the referendum was illusory and that they, in fact, had the masses behind them. The myth, as propagated

Column: 343

by the Barisan Sosialis, is that the referendum was dishonest and rigged. In fact, Sir, you will remember that hilarious moment yesterday when the Member for Queenstown drew attention to the results of the two Gallup polls conducted in Tanjong Pagar and Kreta Ayer by Barisan Sosialis and Communist supporters or dupes in the Nanyang University and the Singapore University. The referendum in which some 600,000 voters participated, he dismissed as unrepresentative. The results of that referendum, he dismissed as unrepresentative of public opinion. But the two Gallup polls conducted by pro-Communist students and dupes supervised by Communists and limited to a few thousand selected subjects in these two areas are now held forward seriously as evidence that these two Gallup polls proved that the Communists had a vast majority of the people behind them. Apparently in these two Gallup polls, well over 90 per cent of the people cast blank votes, implying therefore that the 25 per cent blank votes in the referendum was not truly representative of the people. Surely. Sir, the Member for Queenstown, who is a fully grown adult male, does not expect either this Assembly or the people to take what he says seriously. This business of a private Gallup poll being more reliable than a nationally organised referendum may go down well among the infants who formed the large proportion of the crowd at the Barisan Sosialis rally held a few weeks ago at the Badminton Hall. But surely, Sir, the Member for Queenstown must be on the verge of a mental breakdown if he expects adults to accept this thesis, that his own Gallup poll is more reliable than the nationally organised referendum.

    Anyway, Sir, the Member for Thomson suggested that the referendum was rigged. If the Barisan Sosialis really believe this, then they could very well have taken the matter to Court, produced the evidence and set aside the referendum. But as far as I know, Sir, they have not done so. I know they made art attempt just before polling day

Column: 344

to get the Supreme Court to restrain the Referendum Superintendent from exercising his powers, but the argument was so unconvincing that the Court rejected their plea. But, I think, Sir, there is one authority whose testimony about the honesty, the regularity of the referendum, no single member of the Barisan Sosialis can question. And that authority is none other than Mr Lim Ching Siong. On the night of the 1st of September after polling had closed, Mr Lim Ching Siong was asked by Pressmen to comment on the polling, and this is what he said, and I quote from the Sunday Times of September 2, 1962:

    `Said Mr Lim: "We are sure the majority of the people have cast blank votes in protest against the dishonest referendum."'

    Now, Sir, if Mr Lim Ching Siong had thought that the referendum had been rigged or in any way tampered with, he would not have said so categorically that he was certain that the majority of the people had cast blank votes. If the Barisan Sosialis leaders had thought that the balloting had been so rigged that it would have precluded the people from casting blank votes, the Communists would not have got their followers to camp outside Badminton Hall that night to celebrate a possible victory. If the Barisan Communists had thought that the referendum had been rigged, they would not have waited until the very early hours of the morning to watch the ballot papers being counted and the results announced; nor would they, if they had believed that the balloting had been rigged, have asked for a recount and then scooted off from the Badminton Hall immediately after the counting had begun.

    Sir, the Communists know that the referendum was fairly held. The only trouble is that they really believed their own myth, that their influence over the masses was unassailable. So when they got only a 25 per cent vote which was worse than even their most pessimistic prediction, they knew that the end had come. What angered them even more, I think, Sir, was that the Government had at the last moment decided to accede to the plea that they had made

Column: 345

that the blank votes should be counted separately. I think they would have given their right arm that night for the Government to have decided to count the blank votes with the other votes because then they could have gone out and said, "Yes, you know. But the blank votes exceeded all other votes." But now, once having declared the blank votes separately, it was shown with mathematical accuracy that at their best, the Communists could only muster about 20 to 25 per cent of the electorate, and that from the rural areas where our propaganda had not been able to reach the rural masses. This fact, the realisation that they no longer had the support of the people, has pushed the Communists and the Barisan Sosialis into a new and dangerous course. They knew that they had lost the constitutional struggle in Singapore and that with the advent of Malaysia their chances of winning power constitutionally were gone for good. So they decided on violence. The only way they could halt merger and Malaysia was not by a constitutional struggle in Singapore, but by violence; and the battlefield of violence was first Brunei. Sir, I do not wish to go into details about the involvement of the Barisan Sosialis members and Communists in the armed revolt that took place in Brunei. There is a White Paper on the subject. I think there is a motion when Communist Barisan Sosialis connection with the violent revolt in Brunei would be spelt out. However, the fact to be noted is that the Singapore Communists knew that they could no longer rely on the people of Singapore to fight their anti-Malaysia and anti-merger battle and that the Singapore Communists had now to rely on Azahari and foreign volunteers to fight their anti-Malaysia battle for them. The Singapore Communists and the Barisan Sosialis hoped that the violent struggle in Brunei would then spread to Singapore, and that from Singapore they could provide fuel and support for the violence in Brunei and other parts of Malaysia. If the violence which started in Brunei could spread to Singapore, the Communists hoped that this would give them a chance of imposing, by force, what they could not get the people of

Column: 346

Singapore to do by constitutional means, that is, to break up Malaysia. Fortunately, Sir, the Brunei revolt fizzled out, and so there was one last hope for the so-called anti-colonialists. Just as they went to see certain colonialists in the U.K. Commission to help to break up merger, to come to a deal. So this time, after the revolt in Brunei had fizzled out, they were banking on the fact that relations between Malaya and Indonesia would worsen so that the confrontation would pass from a passive to an active stage when they hoped foreign volunteers and armies would invade Malaysia and help to break up Malaysia.

    It was when the Barisan Sosialis and the Communists not only openly supported violence but were also engaged in playing the treacherous role of supporting intervention by foreign volunteers that the Internal Security Council decided that the Communists now constituted not anti-colonialists but fifth columnists in our midst. These Communists were arrested not because they were our political opponents. We tolerated them for two years. We gave them every opportunity to say or do what they liked. So it was not because they were our political opponents but because they had become fifth columnists - to sell Singapore and Malaysia not only to racketeers, "bums and liars" like Mr Azahari, and like Osmena who says he is prepared to do a deal on Malaysia for something like $10 million. But worse than that. The Barisan Sosialis have become stooges of foreign powers and foreign groups like the Indonesian Communist Party. It is because the people know of this role that they are playing today - that the Barisan Sosialis are not anti-colonialists - that the detention of over 100 Barisan Sosialis supporters has not brought about a single reaction from the people. We know for a fact, Sir, that the Barisan Sosialis leaders on the day some of them were arrested, tried to get some of the unionists like those in the Singapore Bus Workers' Union to go on strike. But this time the people of Singapore had come to know the real role of the Barisan Sosialis - that they were treacherous fifth columnists.

Therefore, when the Barisan Sosialis leaders were locked up, there was not a tear shed.
